Research shows that Black physicians are more likely to accurately assess Black patients' pain tolerance and treat them accordingly (including, for example, prescribing them appropriate amounts of pain medication).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 99" name="FRopinion1.99" href="#FNopinion1.99">99</a></sup>  For high-risk Black newborns, having a Black physician more than doubles the likelihood that the baby will live, and not die.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 100" name="FRopinion1.100" href="#FNopinion1.100">100</a></sup>  Studies also confirm what common sense counsels: Closing wealth<em> </em>disparities through programs like UNC's--which, beyond diversifying the medical profession, open doors to every sort of opportunity--helps address the aforementioned health<em> </em>disparities (in the long run) as well.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 101" name="FRopinion1.101" href="#FNopinion1.101">101</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Do not miss the point that ensuring a diverse student body in higher education helps <em>everyone</em>, not just those who, due to their race, have directly inherited distinct disadvantages with respect to their health, wealth, and wellbeing.  <em>Amici</em> explain that students of every race will come to have a greater appreciation and understanding of civic virtue, democratic values, and our country's commitment to equality.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 102" name="FRopinion1.102" href="#FNopinion1.102">102</a></sup>  The larger economy benefits, too: When it comes down to the brass tacks of dollars and cents, ensuring diversity will, if permitted to work, help save hundreds of billions of dollars annually (by conservative estimates).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 103" name="FRopinion1.103" href="#FNopinion1.103">103</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Thus, we should be celebrating the fact that UNC, once a stronghold of Jim Crow, has now come to understand this.  The flagship educational institution of a former Confederate State has embraced its constitutional obligation to afford genuine equal protection to applicants, and, by extension, to the broader polity that its students will serve after graduation.  Surely that is progress for a university that once engaged in the kind of patently offensive racedominated admissions process that the majority decries.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;With its holistic review process, UNC now treats race as merely one aspect of an applicant's life, when race played a totalizing, all-encompassing, and singularly determinative<em> </em>role for applicants like James for most of this country's history: No matter what else was true about him, being Black meant he had no shot at getting in (the ultimate race-linked uneven playing field).  Holistic programs like UNC's reflect the reality that Black students have only relatively recently been permitted to get into the admissions game at all.  Such programs also reflect universities' clear-eyed optimism that, one day, race <em>will </em>no longer matter.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;So much upside.  Universal benefits ensue from holistic admissions programs that allow consideration of <em>all</em> factors material to merit (including race), and that thereby facilitate diverse student populations.  Once trained, those UNC students who have thrived in the university's diverse learning environment are well equipped to make lasting contributions in a variety of realms and with a variety of colleagues, which, in turn, will steadily decrease the salience of race for future generations.  Fortunately, UNC and other institutions of higher learning are already on this beneficial path.  In fact, all that they have needed to continue moving this country forward (toward full achievement of our Nation's founding promises) is for this Court to get out of the way and let them do their jobs.  To our great detriment, the majority cannot bring itself to do so.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The overarching reason the majority gives for becoming an impediment to racial progress--that its own conception of the Fourteenth Amendment's Equal Protection Clause leaves it no other option--has a wholly self-referential, two-dimensional flatness.  The majority and concurring opinions rehearse this Court's idealistic vision of racial equality, from <em>Brown</em> forward, with appropriate lament for past indiscretions.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 11.  But the race-linked gaps that the law (aided by this Court) previously founded and fostered--which indisputably define our present reality--are strangely absent and do not seem to matter.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;With let-them-eat-cake obliviousness, today, the majority pulls the ripcord and announces "colorblindness for all" by legal fiat.  But deeming race irrelevant in law does not make it so in life.  And having so detached itself from this country's actual past and present experiences, the Court has now been lured into interfering with the crucial work that UNC and other institutions of higher learning are doing to solve America's real-world problems.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;No one benefits from ignorance.  Although formal race-linked legal barriers are gone, race still matters to the lived experiences of all Americans in innumerable ways, and today's ruling makes things worse, not better.  The best that can be said of the majority's perspective is that it proceeds (ostrich-like) from the hope that preventing consideration of race will end racism.  But if that is its motivation, the majority proceeds in vain.  If the colleges of this country are required to ignore a thing that matters, it will not just go away.  It will take <em>longer</em> for racism to leave us.  And, ultimately, ignoring race just makes it matter more.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 104" name="FRopinion1.104" href="#FNopinion1.104">104</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The only way out of this morass--for all of us--is to stare at racial disparity unblinkingly, and then do what evidence and experts tell us is required to level the playing field and march forward together, collectively striving to achieve true equality for all Americans.  It is no small irony that the judgment the majority hands down today will forestall the end of race-based disparities in this country, making the colorblind world the majority wistfully touts much more difficult to accomplish.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">***</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As the Civil War neared its conclusion, General William T. Sherman and Secretary of War Edwin Stanton convened a meeting of Black leaders in Savannah, Georgia.  During the meeting, someone asked Garrison Frazier, the group's spokesperson, what "freedom" meant to him.  He answered, "&nbsp;'placing us where we could reap the fruit of our own labor, and take care of ourselves .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. to have land, and turn it and till it by our own labor.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 105" name="FRopinion1.105" href="#FNopinion1.105">105</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Today's gaps exist because that freedom was denied far longer than it was ever afforded.  Therefore, as <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Sotomayor </small></span>correctly and<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> </small></span>amply explains, UNC's holistic review program pursues a righteous end--legitimate "&nbsp;'because it is defined by the Constitution itself.  The end is the maintenance of freedom.'&nbsp;"  <em>Jones</em> v. <em>Alfred H. Mayer Co.</em>, 392 U.&nbsp;S. 409, 443-444 (1968) (quoting Cong. Globe, 39th Cong., 1st Sess., 1118 (1866) (Rep. Wilson)).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Viewed from this perspective, beleaguered admissions programs such as UNC's are not pursuing a patently unfair, ends-justified ideal of a multiracial democracy at all.  Instead, they are engaged in an earnest effort to secure a more functional one.  The admissions rubrics they have constructed now recognize that an individual's "merit"--his ability to succeed in an institute of higher learning and ultimately contribute something to our society--cannot be fully determined without understanding that individual in full.  There are no special favorites here.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;UNC has thus built a review process that <em>more accurately </em>assesses merit than most of the admissions programs that have existed since this country's founding.  Moreover, in so doing, universities like UNC create pathways to upward mobility for long excluded and historically disempowered racial groups.  Our Nation's history more than justifies this course of action.  And our present reality indisputably  establishes that such programs are still needed--for the general public good--because after centuries of statesanctioned (and enacted) race discrimination, the aforementioned intergenerational race-based gaps in health, wealth, and well-being stubbornly persist.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Rather than leaving well enough alone, today, the majority is having none of it.  Turning back the clock (to a time before the legal arguments and evidence establishing the soundness of UNC's holistic admissions approach existed), the Court indulges those who either do not know our Nation's history or long to repeat it.  Simply put, the race-blind admissions stance the Court mandates from this day forward is unmoored from critical real-life circumstances.  Thus, the Court's meddling not only arrests the noble generational project that America's universities are attempting, it also launches, in effect, a dismally misinformed sociological experiment.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Time will reveal the results.  Yet the Court's own missteps are now both eternally memorialized and excruciatingly plain.  For one thing--based, apparently, on nothing more than Justice Powell's initial say so--it drastically discounts the primary reason that the racial-diversity objectives it excoriates are needed, consigning race-related historical happenings to the Court's own analytical dustbin.  Also, by latching onto arbitrary timelines and professing insecurity about missing metrics, the Court sidesteps unrefuted proof of the compelling benefits of holistic admissions programs that factor in race (hard to do, for there is plenty), simply proceeding as if no such evidence exists.  Then, ultimately, the Court surges to vindicate equality, but Don Quixote style--pitifully perceiving itself as the sole vanguard of legal high ground when, in reality, its perspective is not constitutionally compelled and will hamper the best judgments of our world-class educational institutions about who they need to bring onto their campuses right now to benefit every American, no matter their race.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 106" name="FRopinion1.106" href="#FNopinion1.106">106</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court has come to rest on the bottom-line conclusion that racial diversity in higher education is only worth potentially preserving insofar as it might be needed to prepare Black Americans and other underrepresented minorities for success in the bunker, not the boardroom (a particularly awkward place to land, in light of the history the majority opts to ignore).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 107" name="FRopinion1.107" href="#FNopinion1.107">107</a></sup>  It would be deeply unfortunate if the Equal Protection Clause actually demanded this perverse, ahistorical, and counterproductive outcome.  To impose this result in that Clause's name when it requires no such thing, and to thereby obstruct our collective progress toward the full realization of the Clause's promise, is truly a tragedy for us all.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Together with No. 21-707, <em>Students for Fair Admissions, Inc.</em> v. <em>University of North Carolina et&nbsp;al.</em>, on certiorari before judgment to the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit.<em> </em></p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Jackson</small></span> attempts to minimize the role that race plays in UNC's admissions process by noting that, from 2016-2021, the school accepted a lower "percentage of the most academically excellent in-state Black candidates"--that is,<em> </em>65 out of 67 such applicants (97.01%)--than it did similarly situated Asian applicants--that is,<em> </em>1118 out of 1139 such applicants (98.16%).  <em>Post</em>, at 20 (dissenting opinion); see also 3 App. in No. 21-707, pp. 1078-1080.  It is not clear how the rejection of just two black applicants over five years could be "indicative of a genuinely holistic [admissions] process," as <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Jackson</small></span> contends.  <em>Post</em>, at 20-21.  And indeed it cannot be, as the <em>overall</em> acceptance rates of academically excellent applicants to UNC illustrates full well.  According to SFFA's expert, over 80% of all black applicants in the top academic decile were admitted to UNC, while under 70% of white and Asian applicants in that decile were admitted.  3 App. in No. 21-707, at 1078-1083.  In the second highest academic decile, the disparity is even starker: 83% of black applicants were admitted, while 58% of white applicants and 47% of Asian applicants were admitted.  <em>Ibid.</em>  And in the third highest decile, 77% of black applicants were admitted, compared to 48% of white applicants and 34% of Asian applicants.  <em>Ibid.  </em>The dissent does not dispute the accuracy of these figures.  See <em>post</em>, at 20, n. 94 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Jackson</small></span>, J.).  And its contention that white and Asian students "receive a diversity plus" in UNC's race-based admissions system blinks reality.  <em>Post</em>, at 18. </p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The same is true at Harvard.  See Brief for Petitioner 24 ("[A]n African American [student] in [the fourth lowest academic] decile has a higher chance of admission (12.8%) than an Asian American in the <em>top</em> decile (12.7%)." (emphasis added)); see also 4 App. in No. 20-1199, p. 1793 (black applicants in the top four academic deciles are between four and ten times more likely to be admitted to Harvard than Asian applicants in those deciles).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Title VI provides that "[n]o person in the United States shall, on the ground of race, color, or national origin, be excluded from participation in, be denied the benefits of, or be subjected to discrimination under any program or activity receiving Federal financial assistance."  42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 2000d.  "We have explained that discrimination that violates the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment committed by an institution that accepts federal funds also constitutes a violation of Title VI."  <em>Gratz</em> v. <em>Bollinger</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. 244, 276, n.&nbsp;23 (2003).  Although <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span> questions that proposition, no party asks us to reconsider it.  We accordingly evaluate Harvard's admissions program under the standards of the Equal Protection Clause itself.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The first time we determined that a governmental racial classification satisfied "the most rigid scrutiny" was 10 years before <em>Brown </em>v.<em> Board of Education</em>, 347 U.&nbsp;S. 483 (1954), in the infamous case <em>Korematsu</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 323 U.&nbsp;S. 214, 216 (1944).  There, the Court upheld the internment of "all persons of Japanese ancestry in prescribed West Coast .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. areas" during World War II because "the military urgency of the situation demanded" it.  <em>Id.</em>, at 217, 223.  We have since overruled <em>Korematsu</em>, recognizing that it was "gravely wrong the day it was decided."  <em>Trump</em> v. <em>Hawaii</em>, 585 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2018) (slip op., at 38).  The Court's decision in <em>Korematsu</em> nevertheless "demonstrates vividly that even the most rigid scrutiny can sometimes fail to detect an illegitimate racial classification" and that "[a]ny retreat from the most searching judicial inquiry can only increase the risk of another such error occurring in the future."  <em>Adarand Constructors, Inc. </em>v.<em> Pea</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S. 200, 236 (1995) (internal quotation marks omitted).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The principal dissent, for its part, claims that the Court has also permitted "the use of race when that use burdens minority populations."  <em>Post</em>, at 38-39 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J.).  In support of that claim, the dissent cites two cases that have nothing to do with the Equal Protection Clause.  See <em>ibid.</em> (citing <em>United States</em> v. <em>Brignoni-Ponce</em>, 422 U.&nbsp;S. 873 (1975) (Fourth Amendment case), and <em>United States</em> v. <em>Martinez-Fuerte</em>, 428 U.&nbsp;S. 543 (1976) (another Fourth Amendment case)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The United States as <em>amicus curiae </em>contends that race-based admissions programs further compelling interests at our Nation's military academies.  No military academy is a party to these cases, however, and none of the courts below addressed the propriety of race-based admissions systems in that context.  This opinion also does not address the issue, in light of the potentially distinct interests that military academies may present.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For that reason, one dissent candidly advocates abandoning the demands of strict scrutiny.  See <em>post</em>, at 24, 26-28 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Jackson</small></span>, J.) (arguing the Court must "get out of the way," "leav[e] well enough alone," and defer to universities and "experts" in determining who should be discriminated against).  An opinion professing fidelity to history (to say nothing of the law) should surely see the folly in that approach.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Jackson </small></span>contends<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> </small></span>that race does not play a "determinative role for applicants" to UNC.  <em>Post</em>, at 24.  But even the principal dissent acknowledges that race--and race alone--explains the admissions decisions for hundreds if not thousands of applicants to UNC each year.  <em>Post</em>, at 33, n. 28 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J.); see also <em>Students for Fair Admissions, Inc.</em> v. <em>University of N.&nbsp;C. at Chapel Hill</em>, No. 1:14-cv-954 (MDNC, Dec. 21, 2020), ECF Doc. 233, at 23-27 (UNC expert testifying that race explains 1.2% of in state and 5.1% of out of state admissions decisions); 3 App. in No. 21-707, at 1069 (observing that UNC evaluated 57,225 in state applicants and 105,632 out of state applicants from 2016-2021).  The suggestion by the principal dissent that our analysis relies on extra-record materials, see <em>post</em>, at 29-30, n.&nbsp;25 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J.), is simply mistaken.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The principal dissent claims that "[t]he fact that Harvard's racial shares of admitted applicants varies relatively little .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. is unsurprising and reflects the fact that the racial makeup of Harvard's applicant pool also varies very little over this period."  <em>Post</em>, at 35 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J.) (internal quotation marks omitted).  But that is exactly the point: Harvard must use precise racial preferences year in and year out to maintain the unyielding demographic composition of its class.  The dissent is thus left to attack the numbers themselves, arguing they were "handpicked" "from a truncated period."  <em>Ibid.</em>, n. 29 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J.).  As supposed proof, the dissent notes that the share of Asian students at Harvard varied significantly from 1980 to 1994--a 14-year period that ended nearly three decades ago.  4 App. in No. 20-1199, at 1770.  But the relevance of that observation--handpicked and truncated as it is--is lost on us.  And the dissent does not and cannot dispute that the share of black and Hispanic students at Harvard--"the primary beneficiaries" of its race-based admissions policy--has remained consistent for decades.  397 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d, at 178; 4 App. in No. 20-1199, at 1770.  For all the talk of holistic and contextual judgments, the racial preferences at issue here in fact operate like clockwork.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Perhaps recognizing as much, the principal dissent at one point attempts to press a different remedial rationale altogether, stating that both respondents "have sordid legacies of racial exclusion."  <em>Post</em>, at 21 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J</small></span>.).  Such institutions should perhaps be the very <em>last</em> ones to be allowed to make race-based decisions, let alone be accorded deference in doing so.  In any event, neither university defends its admissions system as a remedy for past discrimination--their own or anyone else's.  See Tr. of Oral Arg. in No. 21-707, at 90 ("[W]e're not pursuing any sort of remedial justification for our policy.").  Nor has any decision of ours permitted a remedial justification for race-based college admissions.  Cf. <em>Bakke</em>, 438 U.&nbsp;S., at 307 (opinion of Powell, J.).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The principal dissent rebukes the Court for not considering adequately the reliance interests respondents and other universities had in <em>Grutter</em>.  But as we have explained, <em>Grutter</em> itself limited the reliance that could be placed upon it by insisting, over and over again, that race-based admissions programs be limited in time.  See <em>supra</em>, at 20.  <em>Grutter </em>indeed went so far as to suggest a specific period of reliance--25 years--precluding the indefinite reliance interests that the dissent articulates.  Cf. <em>post</em>, at 2-4<em> </em>(<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kavanaugh</small></span>, J., concurring).  Those interests are, moreover, vastly overstated on their own terms.  Three out of every five American universities do <em>not </em>consider race in their admissions decisions.  See Brief for Respondent in No. 20-1199, p.&nbsp;40.  And several States--including some of the most populous (California, Florida, and Michigan)--have prohibited race-based admissions outright.  See Brief for Oklahoma et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 9, n.&nbsp;6.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In fact, Indians would not be considered citizens until several decades later.  Indian Citizenship Act of 1924, ch. 233, 43 Stat. 253 (declaring that all Indians born in the United States are citizens).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;There is "some support" in the history of enactment for at least "four interpretations of the first section of the proposed amendment, and in particular of its Privileges [or] Immunities Clause: it would authorize Congress to enforce the Privileges and Immunities Clause of Article IV; it would forbid discrimination between citizens with respect to fundamental rights; it would establish a set of basic rights that all citizens must enjoy; and it would make the Bill of Rights applicable to the states."  D. Currie, The Reconstruction Congress, 75 U. Chi. L.&nbsp;Rev. 383, 406 (2008) (citing sources).  Notably, those four interpretations are all colorblind.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;UNC asserts that the Freedmen's Bureau gave money to Berea College at a time when the school sought to achieve a 50-50 ratio of black to white students.  Brief for University Respondents in No. 21-707, p.&nbsp;32.  But, evidence suggests that, at the relevant time, Berea conducted its admissions without distinction by race.  S. Wilson, Berea College: An Illustrated History 2 (2006) (quoting Berea's first president's statement that the school "would welcome 'all races of men, without distinction'&nbsp;").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court has remarked that Title VI is coextensive with the Equal Protection Clause.  See <em>Gratz</em> v. <em>Bollinger</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. 244, 276, n.&nbsp;23 (2003) ("We have explained that discrimination that violates the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment committed by an institution that accepts federal funds also constitutes a violation of Title VI"); <em>Regents of</em> <em>Univ. of Cal.</em> v. <em>Bakke</em>, 438 U.&nbsp;S. 265, 287 (1978) (opinion of Powell, J.) ("Title VI .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. proscribe[s] only those racial classifications that would violate the Equal Protection Clause").  As <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch </small></span>points out, the language of Title VI makes no allowance for racial considerations in university admissions.  See <em>post</em>, at 2-3 (concurring opinion).  Though I continue to adhere to my view in <em>Bostock</em> v. <em>Clayton County</em>, 590 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___-___ (2020) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 1-54), I agree with <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span>'s concurrence in this case.  The plain text of Title VI reinforces the colorblind view of the Fourteenth Amendment.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In fact, the Massachusetts Supreme Court in 1783 declared that slavery was abolished in Massachusetts by virtue of the newly enacted Constitution's provision of equality under the law.  See <em>The Quock Walker Case</em>, in 1 H. Commager, Documents of American History 110 (9th ed. 1973) (Cushing, C.&nbsp;J.) ("[W]hatever sentiments have formerly prevailed in this particular or slid in upon us by the example of others, a different idea has taken place with the people of America, more favorable to the natural rights of mankind, and to that natural, innate desire of Liberty&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.  And upon this ground our Constitution of Government .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. sets out with declaring that all men are born free and equal .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. and in short is totally repugnant to the idea of being born slaves").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Briefing in a case consolidated with <em>Brown</em> stated the colorblind position forthrightly: Classifications "[b]ased [s]olely on [r]ace or [c]olor" "can never be" constitutional.  Juris. Statement in <em>Briggs </em>v. <em>Elliott</em>, O.&nbsp;T. 1951, No. 273, pp.&nbsp;20-21, 25, 29; see also Juris. Statement in <em>Davis </em>v. <em>County School Bd. of Prince Edward Cty.</em>, O.&nbsp;T. 1952, No. 191, p.&nbsp;8 ("Indeed, we take the unqualified position that the Fourteenth Amendment has totally stripped the state of power to make race and color the basis for governmental action.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.  For this reason alone, we submit, the state separate school laws in this case must fall").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Indeed, the lawyers who litigated <em>Brown</em> were unwilling to take this bet, insisting on a colorblind legal rule.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, Supp. Brief for Appellants on Reargument in Nos. 1, 2, and 4, and for Respondents in No. 10, in <em>Brown</em> v. <em>Board of Education</em>, O.&nbsp;T. 1953, p.&nbsp;65 ("That the Constitution is color blind is our dedicated belief&nbsp;"); Brief for Appellants in <em>Brown</em> v. <em>Board of Education</em>, O.&nbsp;T. 1952, No. 1, p.&nbsp;5 ("The Fourteenth Amendment precludes a state from imposing distinctions or classifications based upon race and color alone").  In fact, Justice Marshall viewed Justice Harlan's <em>Plessy</em> dissent as "a 'Bible' to which he turned during his most depressed moments"; no opinion "buoyed Marshall more in his pre-<em>Brown</em> days."  In Memoriam: Honorable Thurgood Marshall, Proceedings of the Bar and Officers of the Supreme Court of the United States, p.&nbsp;X (1993) (remarks of Judge Motley).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Sotomayor </small></span>rejects this mismatch theory as "debunked long ago," citing an <em>amicus</em> brief.  <em>Post</em>,<em> </em>at 56.  But, in 2016, the Journal of Economic Literature published a review of mismatch literature--coauthored by a critic and a defender of affirmative action--which concluded that the evidence for mismatch was "fairly convincing."  P. Arcidiacono &amp; M. Lovenheim, Affirmative Action and the Quality-Fit Tradeoff, 54 J. Econ. Lit. 3, 20 (Arcidiacono &amp; Lovenheim).  And, of course, if universities wish to refute the mismatch theory, they need only release the data necessary to test its accuracy.  See Brief for Richard Sander as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 16-19 (noting that universities have been unwilling to provide the necessary data concerning student admissions and outcomes); accord, Arcidiacono &amp; Lovenheim 20 ("Our hope is that better datasets soon will become available").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Sotomayor</small></span> apparently believes that race-conscious admission programs can somehow increase the chances that members of certain races (blacks and Hispanics) are admitted without decreasing the chances of admission for members of other races (Asians).  See <em>post</em>, at 58-59.  This simply defies mathematics.  In a zero-sum game like college admissions, any sorting mechanism that takes race into account in any way, see <em>post</em>, at 27 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Jackson, </small></span>J.) (defending such a system), has discriminated based on race to the benefit of some races and the detriment of others.  And, the universities here admit that race is determinative in at least some of their admissions decisions.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, Tr. of Oral Arg. in No. 20-1199, at 67; 567 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d 580, 633 (MDNC 2021); see also 397 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d 126, 178 (Mass. 2019) (noting that, for Harvard, "race is a determinative tip for" a significant percentage "of all admitted African American and Hispanic applicants"); <em>ante</em>, at 5, n.&nbsp;1 (describing the role that race plays in the universities' admissions processes).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Even beyond Asian Americans, it is abundantly clear that the university respondents' racial categories are vastly oversimplistic, as the opinion of the Court and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span>'s concurrence make clear.  See <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 24-25; <em>post</em>,<em> </em>at<em> </em>5-7 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span>,&nbsp;J.).  Their "affirmative action" programs do not help Jewish, Irish, Polish, or other "white"  ethnic groups whose ancestors faced discrimination upon arrival in America, any more than they help the descendants of those JapaneseAmerican citizens interned during World War II.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Again, universities may offer admissions preferences to students from disadvantaged backgrounds, and they need not withhold those preferences from students who happen to be members of racial minorities.  Universities may not, however, assume that all members of certain racial minorities are disadvantaged.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Such black achievement in "racially isolated" environments is neither new nor isolated to higher education.  See T. Sowell, Education: Assumptions Versus History 7-38 (1986).  As I have previously observed, in the years preceding <em>Brown</em>, the "most prominent example of an exemplary black school was Dunbar High School," America's first public high school for black students.  <em>Parents Involved in Community Schools </em>v.<em> Seattle School Dist. No. 1</em>, 551 U.&nbsp;S. 701, 763 (2007) (concurring opinion).  Known for its academics, the school attracted black students from across the Washington, D.&nbsp;C., area.  "[I]n the period 1918-1923, Dunbar graduates earned fifteen degrees from Ivy League colleges, and ten degrees from Amherst, Williams, and Wesleyan."  Sowell, Education: Assumptions Versus History, at 29.  Dunbar produced the first black General in the U.&nbsp;S. Army, the first black Federal Court Judge, and the first black Presidential Cabinet member.  A. Stewart, First Class: The Legacy of Dunbar 2 (2013).  Indeed, efforts towards racial integration ultimately precipitated the school's decline.  When the D.&nbsp;C. schools moved to a neighborhood-based admissions model, Dunbar was no longer able to maintain its prior admissions policies--and "[m]ore than 80 years of quality education came to an abrupt end."  T. Sowell, Wealth, Poverty and Politics 194 (2016).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also A. Qin, Aiming for an Ivy and Trying to Seem 'Less Asian,' N.&nbsp;Y. Times, Dec.&nbsp;3, 2022, p.&nbsp;A18, col.&nbsp;1 ("[T]he rumor that students can appear 'too Asian' has hardened into a kind of received wisdom within many Asian American communities," and "college admissions consultants [have] spoke[n] about trying to steer their Asian American clients away from so-called typically Asian activities such as Chinese language school, piano and Indian classical instruments.").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Though the matter did not receive much attention in the proceedings below, it appears that the Common Application has evolved in recent years to allow applicants to choose among more options to describe their backgrounds.  The decisions below do not disclose how much Harvard or UNC made use of this further information (or whether they make use of it now).  But neither does it make a difference.  Title VI no more tolerates discrimination based on 60 racial categories than it does 6.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also E. Bazelon, Why Is Affirmative Action in Peril? One Man's Decision, N.&nbsp;Y. Times Magazine, Feb. 15, 2023, p.&nbsp;41 ("In the Ivy League, children whose parents are in the top 1 percent of the income distribution are 77 times as likely to attend as those whose parents are in the bottom 20 percent of the income bracket."); <em>ibid.</em> ("[A] common critique .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. is that schools have made a bargain with economic elites of all races, with the exception of Asian Americans, who are underrepresented compared with their level of academic achievement.").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The principal dissent chides me for "reach[ing] beyond the factfinding below" by acknowledging SFFA's argument that other universities have employed various race-neutral tools.  <em>Post,</em> at 29-30, n.&nbsp;25 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J</small></span>.).  Contrary to the dissent's suggestion, however, I do not purport to find facts about those practices; all I do here is recount what SFFA has argued every step of the way.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> Brief for Petitioner 55, 66-67; 1 App. in No. 20-1199, pp. 415-416, 440; 2 App. in No. 21-707, pp. 551-552.  Nor, of course, is it somehow remarkable to acknowledge the parties' arguments.  The principal dissent itself recites SFFA's arguments about Harvard's and other universities' practices too.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>post,</em> at 30-31, 50 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J</small></span>.).  In truth, it is the dissent that reaches beyond the factfinding below when it argues from studies recited in a dissenting opinion in a different case decided almost a decade ago.  <em>Post,</em> at 29-30, n.&nbsp;25 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J.</small></span>); see also <em>post,</em> at 18-21 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J</small></span>.) (further venturing beyond the trial records to discuss data about employment, income, wealth, home ownership, and healthcare).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Brief for Defense of Freedom Institute for Policy Studies as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 11 (recruited athletes make up less than 1% of Harvard's applicant pool but represent more than 10% of the admitted class); P. Arcidiacono, J. Kinsler, &amp; T. Ransom, Legacy and Athlete Preferences at Harvard, 40 J. Lab. Econ. 133, 141, n.&nbsp;17 (2021) (recruited athletes were the only applicants admitted with the lowest possible academic rating and 79% of recruited athletes with the next lowest rating were admitted compared to 0.02% of other applicants with the same rating).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The principal dissent suggests "some Asian American applicants are actually advantaged by Harvard's use of race."  <em>Post, </em>at 60 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J</small></span>.) (internal quotation marks omitted).  What is the dissent's basis for that claim?  The district court's finding that "considering applicants' race <em>may</em> improve the admission chances of <em>some</em> Asian Americans <em>who connect their racial identities with particularly compelling narratives</em>."  397 F. Supp. 3d, at 178 (emphasis added).  The dissent neglects to mention those key qualifications.  Worse, it ignores completely the district court's further finding that "<em>overall</em>" Harvard's race-conscious admissions policy "results in fewer Asian American[s] .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. being admitted."  <em>Ibid</em>. (emphasis added).  So much for affording the district court's "careful factfinding" the "deference it [is] owe[d]."  <em>Post,</em> at 29-30, n.&nbsp;25 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J</small></span>.).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also, <em>e.g.</em>, Tr. of Oral Arg. in No. 20-1199, at 67, 84, 91; Tr. of Oral Arg. in No. 21-707, at 70-71, 81, 84, 91-92, 110.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Messages among UNC admissions officers included statements such as these:  "[P]erfect 2400 SAT All 5 on AP one B in 11th [grade]."  "Brown?!"  "Heck no. Asian."  "Of course.  Still impressive."; "If it[']s brown and above a 1300 [SAT] put them in for [the] merit/Excel [scholarship]."; "I just opened a brown girl who's an 810 [SAT]."; "I'm going through this trouble because this is a bi-racial (black/white) male."; "[S]tellar academics for a Native Amer[ican]/African Amer[ican] kid."  3 App. in No. 21-707, pp. 1242-1251.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Left with no reply on the statute or its application to the facts, the principal dissent suggests that it violates "principles of party presentation" and abandons "judicial restraint" even to look at the text of Title&nbsp;VI.  <em>Post</em>, at 26-27, n.&nbsp;21 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J.</small></span>).  It is a bewildering suggestion.  SFFA sued Harvard and UNC under Title VI.  And when a party seeks relief under a statute, our task is to apply the law's terms as a reasonable reader would have understood them when Congress enacted them.  <em>Bostock </em>v. <em>Clayton County</em>, 590 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2020) (slip op., at 4).  To be sure, parties are free to frame their arguments.  But they are not free to stipulate to a statute's meaning and no party may "waiv[e]" the proper interpretation of the law by "fail[ing] to invoke it."  <em>EEOC</em> v. <em>FLRA</em>, 476 U.&nbsp;S. 19, 23 (1986) (<em>per curiam</em>) (internal quotation marks omitted); see also <em>Young</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 315 U.&nbsp;S. 257, 258-259 (1942).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court's decision will first apply to the admissions process for the college class of 2028, which is the next class to be admitted.  Some might have debated how to calculate <em>Grutter</em>'s 25-year period--whether it ends with admissions for the college class of 2028 or instead for the college class of 2032.  But neither Harvard nor North Carolina argued that <em>Grutter</em>'s 25-year period ends with<em> </em>the class of 2032 rather than the class of 2028.  Indeed, notwithstanding the 25-year limit set forth in <em>Grutter</em>,<em> </em>neither university embraced <em>any </em>temporal limit on race-based affirmative action in higher education, or identified any end date for its continued use of race in admissions.  <em>Ante, </em>at 30-34.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>*<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Jackson</small></span> did not participate in the consideration or decision of the case in No. 20-1199 and joins this opinion only as it applies to the case in No. 21-707.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> acknowledges, the HBCUs, including Howard University, account for a high proportion of Black college graduates.  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 56-57 (concurring opinion).  That reality cannot be divorced from the history of anti-Black discrimination that gave rise to the HBCUs and the targeted work of the Freedmen's Bureau to help Black people obtain a higher education.  See HBCU Brief 13-15.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;By the time the Fourteenth Amendment was ratified by the States in 1868, "education had become a right of state citizenship in the constitution of every readmitted state," including in North Carolina.  D. <br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority suggests that "it required a Second Founding to undo" programs that help ensure racial integration and therefore greater equality in education.  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 38<em>.</em>  At the risk of stating the blindingly obvious, and as <em>Brown</em> recognized, the Fourteenth Amendment was intended to undo the effects of a world where laws systematically subordinated Black people and created a racial caste system.  Cf. <em>Dred Scott </em>v. <em>Sandford</em>, 19 How. 393, 405 (1857).  <em>Brown</em> and its progeny recognized the need to take affirmative, race-conscious steps to eliminate that system.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNopinion1.19" href="#FRopinion1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Dept. of Commerce, Census Bureau, Health Insurance Coverage in the United States: 2021, p.&nbsp;9 (fig. 5); <em>id.</em>, at 29 (Table C-1), https://www. census.gov/library/publications/2022/demo/p60-278.html (noting racial minorities, particularly Latinos, are less likely to have health insurance coverage).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNopinion1.20" href="#FRopinion1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In 1979, prompted by lawsuits filed by civil rights lawyers under Title VI, the U.&nbsp;S. Department of Health, Education, and Welfare "revoked UNC's federal funding for its continued noncompliance" with <em>Brown</em>.  3 App. 1688; see <em>Adams </em>v<em>. Richardson</em>, 351 F.&nbsp;Supp. 636, 637 (DC 1972); <em>Adams </em>v<em>. Califano</em>, 430 F.&nbsp;Supp. 118, 121 (DC 1977).  North Carolina sued the Federal Government in response, and North Carolina Senator Jesse Helms introduced legislation to block federal desegregation efforts.  3 App. 1688<em>.  </em>UNC praised those actions by North Carolina public officials.  <em>Ibid.  </em>The litigation ended in 1981, after the Reagan administration settled with the State.  See <em>North Carolina </em>v<em>. Department of Education</em>, No. 79-217-CIV-5 (EDNC, July 17, 1981) (Consent Decree).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNopinion1.21" href="#FRopinion1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See 1 App. 20-21 (campus climate survey showing <em>inter alia</em> that "91 percent of students heard insensitive or disparaging racial remarks made by other students"); 2 <em>id.</em>, at 1037 (Black student testifying that a white student called him "the N word" and, on a separate occasion at a fraternity party, he was "told that no slaves were allowed in"); <em>id.</em>, at 955 (student testifying that he was "the only African American student in the class," which discouraged him from speaking up about racially salient issues); <em>id.</em>, at 762-763 (student describing that being "the only Latina" made it "hard to speak up" and made her feel "foreign" and "an outsider").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The same standard that applies under the Equal Protection Clause guides the Court's review under Title VI, as the majority correctly recognizes<em>.  </em>See <em>ante</em>, at 6, n.&nbsp;2; see also <em>Regents of Univ. of Cal. </em>v. <em>Bakke</em>, 438 U.&nbsp;S. 265, 325 (1978) (Brennan, J., concurring).  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch </small></span>argues that "Title VI bears independent force" and holds universities to an even higher standard than the Equal Protection Clause.  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 25.  Because no party advances <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch'</small></span>s argument, see <em>ante</em>, at 6, n.&nbsp;2, the Court properly declines to address it under basic principles of party presentation.  See <em>United States </em>v.<em> Sineneng-Smith</em>, 590 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2020) (slip op., at 3).  Indeed, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch'</small></span>s approach calls for even more<em> </em>judicial restraint.  If petitioner could prevail under <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span>'s statutory analysis, there would be no reason for this Court to reach the constitutional question. See <em>Escambia County </em>v<em>. McMillan</em>, 466 U.&nbsp;S. 48, 51 (1984) (<em>per&nbsp;curiam</em>).  In a statutory case, moreover, <em>stare decisis</em> carries "enhanced force," as it would be up to Congress to "correct any mistake it sees" with "our interpretive decisions."  <em>Kimble </em>v<em>. Marvel Entertainment, LLC</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 446, 456 (2015).  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span>&nbsp;wonders why the dissent, like the majority, does not "engage" with his statutory arguments.  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 16<em>.</em>  The answer is simple: This Court plays "the role of neutral arbiter of matters the parties present."  <em>Greenlaw </em>v.<em> United States</em>, 554 U.&nbsp;S. 237, 243 (2008).  Petitioner made a  strategic litigation choice, and in our adversarial system, it is not up to this Court to come up with "wrongs to right" on behalf of litigants.&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Id.</em>,&nbsp;at 244 (internal quotation marks omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNopinion1.23" href="#FRopinion1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;SFFA is a 501(c)(3) nonprofit organization founded after this Court's decision in <em>Fisher I</em>, 570 U.&nbsp;S. 297 (2013).  App. to Pet. for Cert. in No.  20-1199, p.&nbsp;10.  Its original board of directors had three self-appointed members: Edward Blum, Abigail Fisher (the plaintiff in <em>Fisher</em>), and Richard Fisher.  See <em>ibid.</em></p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNopinion1.24" href="#FRopinion1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Bypassing the Fourth Circuit's opportunity to review the District Court's opinion in the <em>UNC</em> case, SFFA sought certiorari before judgment, urging that, "[p]aired with <em>Harvard</em>," the <em>UNC </em>case would "allow the Court to resolve the ongoing validity of race-based admissions under both Title VI and the Constitution."  Pet. for Cert. in No. 21-707, p.&nbsp;27.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Generally speaking, top percentage plans seek to enroll a percentage of the graduating high school students with the highest academic credentials.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Fisher II</em>, 579 U.&nbsp;S., at 373 (describing the University of Texas' Top Ten Percent Plan).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNopinion1.26" href="#FRopinion1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;SFFA and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span> reach beyond the factfinding below and argue that universities in States that have banned the use of race in college admissions have achieved racial diversity through efforts such as increasing socioeconomic preferences, so UNC could do the same.  Brief for Petitioner 85-86; <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 14.  Data from those States disprove that theory.  Institutions in those States experienced "&nbsp;'an immediate and precipitous decline in the rates at which underrepresented-minority students applied . . . were admitted . . . and enrolled.'&nbsp;"  <em>Schuette </em>v.<em> BAMN</em>,  572 U.&nbsp;S. 291, 384-390 (2014) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J., dissenting); see <em>infra</em>,<em> </em>at 63-64.  In addition, UNC "already engages" in race-neutral efforts focused on socioeconomic status, including providing "exceptional levels of financial aid" and "increased and targeted recruiting."  <em>UNC</em>, 567 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d, at 665.</p>
<p><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span> argues that he is simply "recount[ing] what SFFA has argued."  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 14, n.&nbsp;4<em>.  </em>That is precisely the point: SFFA's arguments were not credited by the court below.  "[W]e are a court of review, not of first view."  <em>Cutter </em>v.<em> Wilkinson</em>,<em> </em>544 U.&nbsp;S. 709, 718, n.&nbsp;7 (2005).<em>  </em><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span> also suggests it is inappropriate for the dissent to respond to the majority by relying on materials beyond the findings of fact below.  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 14, n.&nbsp;4<em>.</em>  There would be no need for the dissent to do that if the majority stuck to reviewing the District Court's careful factfinding with the deference it owes to the trial court.  Because the majority has made a different choice, the dissent responds.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNopinion1.27" href="#FRopinion1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;SFFA also argues that Harvard discriminates against Asian American students.  Brief for Petitioner 72-75.  As explained below, this claim does not fit under <em>Grutter</em>'s strict scrutiny framework, and the courts below did not err in rejecting that claim.  See <em>infra</em>,<em> </em>at 59-60.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNopinion1.28" href="#FRopinion1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span> suggests that only "applicants of certain races may receive a 'tip' in their favor."  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 9.  To the extent <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span> means that some races are not eligible to receive a tip based on their race, there is no evidence in the record to support this statement.  Harvard "does not explicitly prioritize any particular racial group over any other and permits its admissions officers to evaluate the racial and ethnic identity of every student in the context of his or her background and circumstances."  <em>Harvard I</em>, 397 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d 126, 190, n.&nbsp;56 (Mass. 2019).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 29" name="FNopinion1.29" href="#FRopinion1.29">29</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Relying on a single footnote in the First Circuit's opinion, the Court claims that Harvard's program is unconstitutional because it "has led to an 11.1% decrease in the number of Asian-Americans admitted to Harvard."  <em>Ante</em>, at 27.  The Court of Appeals, however, merely noted that the United States, at the time represented by a different administration, argued that "absent the consideration of race, [Asian American] representation would increase from 24% to 27%," an 11% increase.  <em>Harvard II</em>, 980 F.&nbsp;3d, at 191, n.&nbsp;29.  Taking those calculations as correct, the Court of Appeals recognized that such an impact from the use of race on the overall makeup of the class is consistent with the impact that this Court's precedents have tolerated.  <em>Ibid</em>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court also notes that "race is determinative for at least some--if not many--of the students" admitted at UNC.  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 27<em>.</em>  The District Court in the <em>UNC</em> case found that "race plays a role in a very small percentage of decisions: 1.2% for in-state students and 5.1% for out-of-state students."  567 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d 580, 634 (MDNC 2021).  The limited use of race at UNC thus has a smaller effect than at Harvard and is also consistent with the Court's precedents.  In addition, contrary to the majority's suggestion, such effect does not prove that "race alone . . . explains the admissions decisions for hundreds if not thousands of applicants to UNC each year."  <em>Ante, </em>at 28, n. 6.  As the District Court found, UNC (like Harvard) "engages a highly individualized, holistic review of each applicant's file, which considers race flexibly as a 'plus factor' as one among many factors in its individualized consideration of each and every applicant."  567 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d, at 662; see <em>id.,</em> at 658 (finding that UNC "rewards different kinds of diversity, and evaluates a candidate within  the context of their lived experience"); <em>id.,</em> at 659 ("The parties stipulated, and the evidence shows, that readers evaluate applicants by taking into consideration dozens of criteria," and even SFFA's expert "concede[d] that the University's admissions process is individualized and holistic").  Stated simply, race is not "a defining feature of any individual application."  <em>Id.,</em> at 662; see also <em>infra</em>, at 48.   </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 30" name="FNopinion1.30" href="#FRopinion1.30">30</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority does not dispute that it has handpicked data from a truncated period, ignoring the broader context of that data and what the data reflect.  Instead, the majority insists that its selected data prove that Harvard's "precise racial preferences" "operate like clockwork."  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 31-32, n.&nbsp;7.  The Court's conclusion that such racial preferences must be responsible for an "unyielding demographic composition of [the]  class," <em>ibid.</em>, misunderstands basic principles of statistics.  A number of factors (most notably, the demographic composition of the applicant pool) affect the demographic composition of the entering class.  Assume, for example, that Harvard admitted students based solely on standardized test scores.  If test scores followed a normal distribution (even with different averages by race) and were relatively constant over time, and if the racial shares of total applicants were also relatively constant over time, one would expect the same "unyielding demographic composition of [the] class."  <em>Ibid.  </em>That would be true even though, under that hypothetical scenario, Harvard does not consider race in admissions at all.  In other words, the Court's inference that precise racial preferences must be the cause of relatively constant racial shares of admitted students is specious.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 31" name="FNopinion1.31" href="#FRopinion1.31">31</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In the context of policies that "benefit rather than burden the minority," the Court has adhered to a strict scrutiny framework despite multiple Members of this Court urging that "the mandate of the Equal Protection Clause" favors applying a less exacting standard of review.  <em>Schuette</em>, 572 U.&nbsp;S., at 373-374 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J., dissenting) (collecting cases).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 32" name="FNopinion1.32" href="#FRopinion1.32">32</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court's "dictum" that Mexican appearance can be one of many factors rested on now-outdated quantitative premises.  <em>United States </em>v.<em> Montero-Camargo</em>, 208 F.&nbsp;3d 1122, 1132 (CA9 2000).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 33" name="FNopinion1.33" href="#FRopinion1.33">33</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span> agrees that the effects from the legacy of slavery and Jim Crow continue today, citing Justice Marshall's opinion in <em>Bakke</em>.  <em>Ante</em>, at 7 (citing 438 U.&nbsp;S., at 395-402).  As explained above, Justice Marshall's view was that <em>Bakke</em> covered only a portion of the Fourteenth Amendment's sweeping reach, such that the Court's higher education precedents must be expanded, not constricted.  See 438 U.&nbsp;S.,  at 395-402 (opinion dissenting in part).  Justice Marshall's reading of the Fourteenth Amendment does not support <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh's </small></span>and the majority's<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> </small></span>opinions.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 34" name="FNopinion1.34" href="#FRopinion1.34">34</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;There is no dispute that respondents' compelling diversity objectives are "substantial, long-standing, and well documented."  <em>UNC</em>, 567 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d, at 655; <em>Harvard II</em>, 980 F.&nbsp;3d, at 186-187.  SFFA did not dispute below that respondents have a compelling interest in diversity.  See <em>id.</em>, at 185; <em>Harvard I</em>, 397 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d, at 133; Tr. of Oral Arg. in No. 21-707, p.&nbsp;121.  And its expert agreed that valuable educational benefits flow from diversity, including richer and deeper learning, reduced bias, and more creative problem solving.  2 App. in No. 21-707, p.&nbsp;546.  SFFA's counsel also emphatically disclaimed the issue at trial.  2 App.&nbsp;in No. 20-1199, p.&nbsp;548 ("Diversity and its benefits are not on trial here").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 35" name="FNopinion1.35" href="#FRopinion1.35">35</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court suggests that promoting the Fourteenth Amendment's vision of equality is a "radical" claim of judicial power and the equivalent of "pick[ing] winners and losers based on the color of their skin."  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 38<em>.</em>  The law sometimes requires consideration of race to achieve racial equality.  Just like drawing district lines that comply with the Voting Rights Act may require consideration of race along with other demographic factors, achieving racial diversity in higher education requires consideration of race along with "age, economic status, religious and political persuasion, and a variety of other demographic factors."  <em>Shaw </em>v<em>. Reno</em>, 509 U.&nbsp;S. 630, 646 (1993) ("[R]ace consciousness does not lead inevitably to impermissible race discrimination").  Moreover, in ordering the admission of Black children to all-white schools "with all deliberate speed" in <em>Brown </em>v. <em>Board of Education</em>, 349 U.&nbsp;S. 294, 301 (1955), this Court did not decide that the Black children should receive an "advantag[e] . . . at the expense of" white children.  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 27<em>. </em> It simply enforced the Equal Protection Clause by leveling the playing field.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 36" name="FNopinion1.36" href="#FRopinion1.36">36</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Today's decision is likely to generate a plethora of litigation by disappointed college applicants who think their credentials and personal qualities should have secured them admission.  By inviting those challenges, the Court's opinion promotes chaos and incentivizes universities to convert their admissions programs into inflexible systems focused on mechanical factors, which will harm all students.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 37" name="FNopinion1.37" href="#FRopinion1.37">37</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court suggests that the term "Asian American" was developed by respondents because they are "uninterested" in whether Asian American students "are adequately represented."  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 25; see also <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 5 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span>, J., concurring) (suggesting that "[b]ureaucrats" devised a system that grouped all Asian Americans into a single racial category).  That argument offends the history of that term.  "The term 'Asian American' was coined in the late 1960s by Asian American activists--mostly college students--to unify Asian ethnic groups that shared common experiences of race-based violence and discrimination and to advocate for civil rights and visibility."  Brief for Asian American Legal Defense and Education Fund et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 9 (AALDEF Brief&nbsp;).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 38" name="FNopinion1.38" href="#FRopinion1.38">38</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span>'s reading, in particular, is quite puzzling.  Unlike the majority, which concludes that respondents' programs should have an end point, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span> suggests that <em>Grutter</em> itself has an expiration date.  He agrees that racial inequality persists, <em>ante</em>, at 7-8, but at the same time suggests that race-conscious affirmative action was only necessary in "another generation," <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 4.  He attempts to analogize expiration dates of court-ordered injunctions in desegregation cases, <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 5,<em> </em>but an expiring injunction does not eliminate the underlying constitutional principle.  His musings about different college classes, <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 7, n.&nbsp;1, are also entirely beside the point.  Nothing in <em>Grutter</em>'s analysis turned on whether someone was applying for the class of 2028 or 2032.  That reading of <em>Grutter </em>trivializes the Court's precedent by reducing it to an exercise in managing academic calendars.  <em>Grutter</em> is no such thing.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 39" name="FNopinion1.39" href="#FRopinion1.39">39</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Before 2018, Harvard's admissions procedures were silent on the use of race in connection with the personal rating.  <em>Harvard II</em>, 980 F.&nbsp;3d, at 169.  Harvard later modified its instructions to say explicitly that "&nbsp;'an applicant's race or ethnicity should not be considered in assigning the personal rating.'&nbsp;"  <em>Ibid.</em></p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 40" name="FNopinion1.40" href="#FRopinion1.40">40</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;At Harvard, "Asian American applicants are accepted at the same rate as other applicants and now make up more than 20% of Harvard's admitted classes," even though "only about 6% of the United States population is Asian American."  <em>Harvard I</em>, 397 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d, at 203.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 41" name="FNopinion1.41" href="#FRopinion1.41">41</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;K. Schaeffer, Pew Research Center, The Changing Face of Congress in 8 Charts (Feb. 7, 2023).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 42" name="FNopinion1.42" href="#FRopinion1.42">42</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See J. Martelli &amp; P. Abels, The Education of a Leader: Educational Credentials and Other Characteristics of Chief Executive Officers, J. of Educ. for Bus. 216 (2010); see also J. Moody, Where the Top Fortune 500 CEOs Attended College, U.&nbsp;S. News &amp; World Report (June 16, 2021).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 43" name="FNopinion1.43" href="#FRopinion1.43">43</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Racial inequality in the pipeline to this institution, too, will deepen.  See J. Fogel, M. Hoopes, &amp; G. Liu, Law Clerk Selection and Diversity: Insights From Fifty Sitting Judges of the Federal Courts of Appeals 7-8  (2022) (noting that from 2005 to 2017, 85% of Supreme Court law clerks were white, 9% were Asian American, 4% were Black, and 1.5% were Latino, and about half of all clerks during that period graduated from two law schools: Harvard and Yale); Brief for American Bar Association as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 25 (noting that more than 85% of lawyers, more than 70% of Article III judges, and more than 80% of state judges in the United States are white, even though white people represent about 60% of the population).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 92" name="FNopinion1.92" href="#FRopinion1.92">92</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A reader might miss this because the majority does not bother to drill down on how UNC's holistic admissions process operates.  Perhaps that explains its failure to apprehend (by reviewing the evidence presented at trial) that everyone, no matter their race, is eligible for a  diversity-linked plus.  Compare <em>ante</em>, at 5, and n.&nbsp;1, with 3 App. 1416, and <em>supra</em>, at 17.  The majority also repeatedly mischaracterizes UNC's holistic admissions-review process as a "race-based admissions system," and insists that UNC's program involves "separating students on the basis of race" and "pick[ing only certain] races to benefit."  <em>Ante</em>, at 5, and n.&nbsp;1, 26, 38.  These claims would be concerning if they had any basis in the record.  The majority appears to have misunderstood (or categorically rejected) the established fact that UNC treats race as merely one of the many aspects of an applicant that, in the real world, matter to understanding the whole person.  Moreover, its holistic review process involves reviewing a wide variety of personal criteria, not just race.  Every applicant competes against thousands of other applicants, each of whom has personal qualities that are taken into account and that other applicants do not--and could not--have.  Thus, the elimination of the race-linked<em> </em>plus would <em>still</em> leave SFFA's members competing against thousands of other applicants to UNC, each of whom has potentially plus-conferring qualities that a given SFFA member does not.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 93" name="FNopinion1.93" href="#FRopinion1.93">93</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See 3 App. 1409, 1414, 1416.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 94" name="FNopinion1.94" href="#FRopinion1.94">94</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Id.</em>, at 1414-1415.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 95" name="FNopinion1.95" href="#FRopinion1.95">95</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See 567 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d, at 617, 619; 3 App. 1078-1080.  The majority cannot deny this factual finding.  Instead, it conducts its own back-of-the-envelope calculations (its numbers appear nowhere in the District Court's opinion) regarding "the <em>overall </em>acceptance rates of academically excellent applicants to UNC," in an effort to trivialize the District Court's conclusion.  <em>Ante</em>, at 5, n.&nbsp;1.  I am inclined to stick with the District Court's findings over the majority's unauthenticated calculations.  Even when the majority's ad&nbsp;hoc statistical analysis is taken at face value, it hardly supports what the majority wishes to intimate: that Black students are being admitted based on UNC's myopic focus on "race--and race alone."  <em>Ante</em>, at 28, n.&nbsp;6.  As the District Court observed, if these Black students "were largely defined in the admissions process by their race, one would expect to find that <em>every</em>" such student "demonstrating academic excellence .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. would be admitted."  567 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d, at 619 (emphasis added).  Contrary to the majority's narrative, "race does not even act as a tipping point for some students with otherwise exceptional qualifications."  <em>Ibid.  </em>Moreover, as the District Court also found, UNC does not even use the bespoke "academic excellence" metric that SFFA's expert "&nbsp;'invented'&nbsp;" for this litigation.  <em>Id.</em>, at 617, 619; see also <em>id.</em>, at 624-625.  The majority's calculations of overall acceptance rates by race on <em>that </em>metric bear scant relationship to, and thus are no indictment of, how UNC's admissions process actually works (a recurring theme in its opinion).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 104" name="FNopinion1.104" href="#FRopinion1.104">104</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas'</small></span>s prolonged attack, <em>ante</em>, at 49-55 (concurring opinion), responds to a dissent I did not write in order to assail an admissions program that is not the one UNC has crafted.  He does not dispute any historical or present fact about the origins and continued existence of race-based disparity (nor could he), yet is somehow persuaded that these realities have no bearing on a fair assessment of "individual achievement," <em>ante</em>, at 51.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas'</small></span>s opinion also demonstrates an obsession with race consciousness that far outstrips my or UNC's holistic understanding that race can be a factor that affects applicants' unique life experiences.  How else can one explain his detection of "an organizing principle based on race," a claim that our society is "fundamentally racist," and a desire for Black "victimhood" or racial "silo[s]," <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 49-52, in this dissent's approval of an admissions program that advances all Americans' shared pursuit of true equality by treating race "on par with" other aspects of identity, <em>supra</em>, at 18?  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas </small></span>ignites too many more straw men to list, or fully extinguish, here.  The takeaway is that those who demand that no one think about race (a classic pink-elephant paradox) refuse to see, much less solve for, the elephant in the room--the race-linked disparities that continue to impede achievement of our great Nation's full potential.  Worse still, by insisting that obvious truths be ignored, they prevent our problem-solving institutions from directly addressing the real import and impact of "social racism" and  "government-imposed racism," <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 55 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas, J.</small></span>, concurring), thereby deterring our collective progression toward becoming a society where race no longer matters.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 105" name="FNopinion1.105" href="#FRopinion1.105">105</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Foner 179.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 106" name="FNopinion1.106" href="#FRopinion1.106">106</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Sotomayor </small></span>has fully explained why the majority's analysis is legally erroneous and how UNC's holistic review program is entirely consistent with the Fourteenth Amendment.  My goal here has been to highlight the interests at stake and to show that holistic admissions programs that factor in race are warranted, just, and universally beneficial.  All told, the Court's myopic misunderstanding of what the Constitution permits will impede what experts and evidence tell us is required (as a matter of social science) to solve for pernicious race-based inequities that are themselves rooted in the persistent denial of equal protection.  "[T]he potential consequences of the [majority's] approach, as measured against the Constitution's objectives .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. provides further reason to believe that the [majority's] approach is legally unsound."  <em>Parents Involved in Community Schools</em> v. <em>Seattle School Dist. No. 1</em>, 551 U.&nbsp;S. 701, 858 (2007) (Breyer, J., dissenting).  I fear that the Court's folly brings our Nation to the brink of coming "full circle" once again.  <em>Regents of Univ. of Cal.</em> v. <em>Bakke</em>, 438 U.&nbsp;S. 265, 402 (1978) (opinion of Marshall, J.).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 107" name="FNopinion1.107" href="#FRopinion1.107">107</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Compare <em>ante</em>, at 22, n.&nbsp;4, with <em>ante</em>, at 22-30, and <em>supra</em>, at 3-4, and nn. 2-3.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

</div>
